Mars[![Image 1: Orange-brown globe with white snow caps](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Mars_-_August_30_2021_-_Flickr_-_Kevin_M._Gill.png/330px-Mars_-_August_30_2021_-_Flickr_-_Kevin_M._Gill.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Mars_-_August_30_2021_-_Flickr_-_Kevin_M._Gill.png)

Mars in true color,[[a]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-1) as captured by the _[Hope](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Emirates\_Mars\_Mission "Emirates Mars Mission")_ orbiter. The [Tharsis Montes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tharsis_Montes "Tharsis Montes") can be seen at the center, with [Olympus Mons](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Olympus_Mons "Olympus Mons") just to the left and [Valles Marineris](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Valles_Marineris "Valles Marineris") at the right.

**Mars** is the fourth [planet](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planet "Planet") from the [Sun](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun "Sun"). It is also known as the "**Red Planet**", because of [its orange-red](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars_surface_color "Mars surface color") appearance.[[22]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-Rees2012-24)[[23]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-nasa_hematite-25) Mars is a desert-like [rocky planet](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rocky_planet "Rocky planet") with a tenuous [carbon dioxide](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carbon_dioxide "Carbon dioxide") (CO 2) [atmosphere](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Atmosphere_of_Mars "Atmosphere of Mars"). At the average surface level the atmospheric pressure is a few thousandths of Earth's, atmospheric temperature ranges from −153 to 20°C (−243 to 68°F)[[24]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-p167-26) and [cosmic radiation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cosmic_radiation "Cosmic radiation") is high. Mars retains some water, [in the ground](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Groundwater_on_Mars "Groundwater on Mars") as well as thinly in the atmosphere, forming [cirrus clouds](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cirrus_cloud "Cirrus cloud"), frost, larger polar regions of [permafrost](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Permafrost "Permafrost") and [ice caps](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Martian_polar_ice_caps "Martian polar ice caps") (with seasonal CO 2 snow), but no liquid surface water. Its surface gravity is roughly a third of Earth's or double that of the Moon. It is half [as wide as](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_Solar_System_objects_by_size "List of Solar System objects by size") Earth or twice the Moon, with a diameter of 6,779 km (4,212 mi), and has a surface area the size of all the dry land of Earth.

[Fine dust](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Martian_regolith#Atmospheric_dust "Martian regolith") is prevalent across the surface and the atmosphere, being picked up and spread at the low Martian gravity even by the weak wind of the tenuous atmosphere. The terrain of Mars roughly follows a north-south divide, the [Martian dichotomy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Martian_dichotomy "Martian dichotomy"), with the northern hemisphere mainly consisting of relatively flat, low lying [plains](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Plain "Plain"), and the southern hemisphere of cratered [highlands](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Highland "Highland"). [Geologically](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Geology_of_Mars "Geology of Mars"), the planet is fairly active with [marsquakes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Marsquake "Marsquake") trembling underneath the ground, but also hosts many enormous extinct [volcanoes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Volcano "Volcano") (the tallest is [Olympus Mons](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Olympus_Mons "Olympus Mons"), 21.9 km or 13.6 mi tall) and one of the largest [canyons](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Canyon "Canyon") in the Solar System ([Valles Marineris](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Valles_Marineris "Valles Marineris"), 4,000 km or 2,500 mi long). Mars has [two natural satellites](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moons_of_Mars "Moons of Mars") that are small and irregular in shape: [Phobos](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Phobos_(moon) "Phobos (moon)") and [Deimos](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Deimos_(moon) "Deimos (moon)"). With a significant [axial tilt](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Axial_tilt "Axial tilt") of 25 degrees Mars experiences [seasons](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Season "Season"), like Earth (which has an axial tilt of 23.5 degrees). A [Martian solar year](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Timekeeping_on_Mars "Timekeeping on Mars") is equal to 1.88 Earth years (687 Earth days), a Martian solar day ([sol](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars_sol "Mars sol")) is equal to 24.6 hours.

Mars was formed approximately 4.5 billion years ago. During the [Noachian](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Noachian "Noachian") period (4.5 to 3.5 billion years ago), its surface was marked by [meteor impacts](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Impact_event "Impact event"), valley formation, erosion, the [possible presence of water oceans](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars_ocean_theory "Mars ocean theory") and the loss of its [magnetosphere](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Magnetosphere "Magnetosphere"). The [Hesperian](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hesperian "Hesperian") period (beginning 3.5 billion years ago and ending 3.3–2.9 billion years ago) was dominated by widespread [volcanic activity](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Volcanism "Volcanism") and flooding that carved immense [outflow channels](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Outflow_channels "Outflow channels"). The [Amazonian](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Amazonian_(Mars) "Amazonian (Mars)") period, which continues to the present is the currently dominating and remaining [influence on geological processes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Aeolian_processes "Aeolian processes"). Due to Mars's geological history, the possibility of past or present [life on Mars](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Life_on_Mars "Life on Mars") remains an area of active scientific investigation.

Being [visible](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Apparent_magnitude "Apparent magnitude") with the naked eye in Earth's sky as a red [wandering star](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Classical_planet "Classical planet"), Mars has been [observed throughout history](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Mars_observation "History of Mars observation"), acquiring diverse associations in different cultures. In 1963 the first flight to Mars took place with [Mars 1](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars_1 "Mars 1"), but communication was lost en route. The first successful flyby [exploration of Mars](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Exploration_of_Mars "Exploration of Mars") was conducted in 1965 with [Mariner 4](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mariner_4 "Mariner 4"). In 1971 [Mariner 9](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mariner_9 "Mariner 9") entered orbit around Mars, being the first spacecraft to orbit any body other than the Moon, Sun or Earth; following in the same year were the first uncontrolled impact ([Mars 2](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars_2 "Mars 2")) and first landing ([Mars 3](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars_3 "Mars 3")) on Mars. Probes have been active on Mars continuously since 1997; at times, more than ten [probes have simultaneously operated](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_missions_to_Mars "List of missions to Mars") in orbit or on the surface, more than at any other planet beside Earth. Mars is an often proposed target for future [human exploration missions](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_mission_to_Mars "Human mission to Mars"), though no such mission is planned yet.

Natural history
---------------

Scientists have theorized that during the [Solar System's formation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Formation_and_evolution_of_the_Solar_System "Formation and evolution of the Solar System"), Mars was created as the result of a [random process](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Stochastic_process "Stochastic process") of run-away accretion of material from the [protoplanetary disk](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Protoplanetary_disk "Protoplanetary disk") that orbited the Sun. Mars has many distinctive chemical features caused by its position in the Solar System. Elements with comparatively low boiling points, such as [chlorine](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chlorine "Chlorine"), [phosphorus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Phosphorus "Phosphorus"), and [sulfur](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sulfur "Sulfur"), are much more common on Mars than on Earth; these elements were probably pushed outward by the young Sun's energetic [solar wind](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Solar_wind "Solar wind").[[25]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-ssr96_1_4_197-27)

After the formation of the planets, the inner Solar System may have been subjected to the so-called [Late Heavy Bombardment](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Late_Heavy_Bombardment "Late Heavy Bombardment"). About 60% of the surface of Mars shows a record of impacts from that era,[[26]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-zharkov93-28)[[27]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-icarus165_1-29)[[28]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-barlow88-30) whereas much of the remaining surface is probably underlain by immense impact basins caused by those events. However, more recent modeling has disputed the existence of the Late Heavy Bombardment.[[29]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-31) There is evidence of an enormous impact basin in the Northern Hemisphere of Mars, spanning 10,600 by 8,500 kilometres (6,600 by 5,300 mi), or roughly four times the size of the Moon's [South Pole–Aitken basin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/South_Pole%E2%80%93Aitken_basin "South Pole–Aitken basin"), which would be the largest impact basin yet discovered if confirmed.[[30]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-northcratersn-32) It has been hypothesized that the basin was formed when Mars was struck by a [Pluto](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pluto "Pluto")-sized body about four billion years ago. The event, thought to be the cause of the [Martian hemispheric dichotomy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Martian_dichotomy "Martian dichotomy"), created the smooth [Borealis basin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Borealis_basin "Borealis basin") that covers 40% of the planet.[[31]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-sciam080627-33)[[32]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-nyt080626-34)

A 2023 study shows evidence, based on the [orbital inclination](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Orbital_inclination "Orbital inclination") of [Deimos](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Deimos_(moon) "Deimos (moon)") (a small [moon](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon "Moon") of Mars), that Mars may once have had a [ring system](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ring_system "Ring system") 3.5 billion years to 4 billion years ago.[[33]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-35) This ring system may have been formed from a moon, 20 times more [massive](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mass "Mass") than [Phobos](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Phobos_(moon) "Phobos (moon)"), orbiting Mars billions of years ago; and Phobos would be a remnant of that ring.[[34]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-36)[[35]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-37)

The geological history of Mars can be split into many periods, but the following are the three primary periods:[[36]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-jog91-38)[[37]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-ssr_96_1_4-39)

*   [Noachian](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Noachian "Noachian") period: Formation of the oldest extant surfaces of Mars, 4.5 to 3.5 billion years ago. Noachian age surfaces are scarred by many large impact craters. The [Tharsis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tharsis "Tharsis") bulge, a volcanic upland, is thought to have formed during this period, with extensive flooding by liquid water late in the period. Named after [Noachis Terra](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Noachis_Terra "Noachis Terra").[[38]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-:0-40)
*   [Hesperian](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hesperian "Hesperian") period: 3.5 to between 3.3 and 2.9 billion years ago. The Hesperian period is marked by the formation of extensive lava plains. Named after [Hesperia Planum](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hesperia_Planum "Hesperia Planum").[[38]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-:0-40)
*   [Amazonian](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Amazonian_(Mars) "Amazonian (Mars)") period: between 3.3 and 2.9 billion years ago to the present. Amazonian regions have few [meteorite impact](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Impact_event "Impact event") craters but are otherwise quite varied. [Olympus Mons](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Olympus_Mons "Olympus Mons") formed during this period, with lava flows elsewhere on Mars. Named after [Amazonis Planitia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Amazonis_Planitia "Amazonis Planitia").[[38]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-:0-40)

Geological activity is still taking place on Mars. The [Athabasca Valles](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Athabasca_Valles "Athabasca Valles") is home to sheet-like lava flows created about 200 million years ago. Water flows in the [grabens](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graben "Graben") called the [Cerberus Fossae](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cerberus_Fossae "Cerberus Fossae") occurred less than 20 million years ago, indicating equally recent volcanic intrusions.[[39]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-ag44_4-41) The _[Mars Reconnaissance Orbiter](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars\_Reconnaissance\_Orbiter "Mars Reconnaissance Orbiter")_ has captured images of avalanches.[[40]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-42)[[41]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-43)

Physical characteristics
------------------------

[![Image 3](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/Terrestrial_planet_size_comp_2024.png/500px-Terrestrial_planet_size_comp_2024.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Terrestrial_planet_size_comp_2024.png)

Mars depicted to scale alongside the [planetary-mass objects](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planetary-mass_object "Planetary-mass object") of the [Inner Solar System](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Inner_Solar_System "Inner Solar System"). From left: [Mercury](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mercury_(planet) "Mercury (planet)"), [Venus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Venus "Venus"), [Earth](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth "Earth"), the [Moon](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon "Moon"), Mars and [Ceres](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ceres_(dwarf_planet) "Ceres (dwarf planet)").

Mars is approximately half the diameter of Earth or twice that of the Moon, with a surface area only slightly less than the total area of Earth's dry land.[[2]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-NasaFactSheet-3) Mars is less dense than Earth, having about 15% of Earth's volume and 11% of Earth's [mass](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mass "Mass"), resulting in about 38% of [Earth's surface gravity](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gravity_of_Earth "Gravity of Earth"). Mars is the only presently known example of a [desert planet](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Desert_planet "Desert planet"), a rocky planet with a surface akin to that of Earth's [deserts](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Desert "Desert"). The red-orange appearance of the Martian surface is caused by [iron(III) oxide](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iron(III)_oxide "Iron(III) oxide") ([nanophase](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nanophase "Nanophase") Fe 2 O 3) and the [iron(III) oxide-hydroxide](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iron(III)_oxide-hydroxide "Iron(III) oxide-hydroxide") mineral [goethite](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Goethite "Goethite").[[42]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-rust-44) It can look like [butterscotch](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Butterscotch "Butterscotch");[[43]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-ismars-45) other common surface colors include golden, brown, tan, and greenish, depending on the [minerals](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mineral "Mineral") present.[[43]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-ismars-45)

### Internal structure

[![Image 4](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Mars_Internal_Structure_%282024%29.svg/330px-Mars_Internal_Structure_%282024%29.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Mars_Internal_Structure_(2024).svg)

Internal structure of Mars[[44]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-St%C3%A4hler-46)[[45]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-2023Natur.622..712S-47)

Mapped gravity field of Mars

Like Earth, Mars is [differentiated](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planetary_differentiation "Planetary differentiation") into a dense metallic [core](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planetary_core "Planetary core") overlaid by less dense rocky layers.[[46]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-Nimmo_2005-48)[[47]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-mars-in-depth-49) The outermost layer is the crust, which is on average about 42–56 kilometres (26–35 mi) thick,[[48]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-2023GeoRL..5003482K-50) with a minimum thickness of 6 kilometres (3.7 mi) in [Isidis Planitia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Isidis_Planitia "Isidis Planitia"), and a maximum thickness of 117 kilometres (73 mi) in the southern [Tharsis plateau.](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tharsis "Tharsis")[[49]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-2022JGRE..12707298W-51) For comparison, Earth's crust averages 27.3 ± 4.8 km in thickness.[[50]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-2013GGG....14.2003H-52) The most abundant elements in the Martian [crust](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Crust_(geology) "Crust (geology)") are [silicon](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Silicon "Silicon"), [oxygen](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Oxygen "Oxygen"), [iron](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iron "Iron"), [magnesium](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Magnesium "Magnesium"), [aluminium](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Aluminium "Aluminium"), [calcium](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Calcium "Calcium"), and [potassium](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Potassium "Potassium"). Mars is confirmed to be seismically active;[[51]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-2023GeoRL..5003619F-53) in 2019, it was reported that [InSight](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/InSight "InSight") had detected and recorded over 450 [marsquakes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Marsquake "Marsquake") and related events.[[52]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-2020NatCo..11.1014G-54)[[53]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-2020NatGe..13..183B-55)

Beneath the crust is a silicate [mantle](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mantle_(geology) "Mantle (geology)") responsible for many of the [tectonic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tectonics_of_Mars "Tectonics of Mars") and [volcanic features](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Volcanology_of_Mars "Volcanology of Mars") on the planet's surface. The upper Martian mantle is a [low-velocity zone](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Low-velocity_zone "Low-velocity zone"), where the velocity of seismic waves is lower than surrounding depth intervals. The mantle appears to be rigid down to the depth of about 250 km,[[45]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-2023Natur.622..712S-47) giving Mars a very thick [lithosphere](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lithosphere "Lithosphere") compared to Earth. Below this the mantle gradually becomes more ductile, and the seismic wave velocity starts to grow again.[[54]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-Khan2021-56) The Martian mantle does not appear to have a thermally insulating layer analogous to Earth's [lower mantle](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lower_mantle "Lower mantle"); instead, below 1050 km in depth, it becomes mineralogically similar to Earth's [transition zone](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Transition_zone_(Earth) "Transition zone (Earth)").[[44]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-St%C3%A4hler-46) At the bottom of the mantle lies a basal [liquid silicate layer](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Magma_ocean "Magma ocean") approximately 150–180 km thick.[[45]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-2023Natur.622..712S-47)[[55]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-2023Natur.622..718K-57)

Mars's [iron and nickel](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iron%E2%80%93nickel_alloy "Iron–nickel alloy") core is completely molten, with no solid inner core.[[56]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-2023Natur.619..733L-58)[[57]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-ArsTechnicaOn2023Natur.619..733L-59) It is around half of Mars's radius, approximately 1650–1675 km, and is enriched in light elements such as [sulfur](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sulfur "Sulfur"), oxygen, [carbon](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carbon "Carbon"), and [hydrogen](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hydrogen "Hydrogen").[[58]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-10.1038/d41586-023-03151-x-60)[[59]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-10.1038/d41586-023-03271-4-61) The temperature of the core is estimated to be 2000–2400 K,[[60]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-62) compared to 5400-6230 K for [Earth's solid inner core](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth%27s_inner_core "Earth's inner core").

### Surface geology

Mars is a [terrestrial planet](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Terrestrial_planet "Terrestrial planet") with a surface that consists of minerals containing [silicon](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Silicon "Silicon") and oxygen, [metals](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Metal "Metal"), and other elements that typically make up [rock](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rock_(geology) "Rock (geology)"). The Martian surface is primarily composed of [tholeiitic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tholeiitic_magma_series "Tholeiitic magma series")[basalt](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Basalt "Basalt"),[[61]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-science324_5928_736-63) although parts are more [silica](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Silica "Silica")-rich than typical basalt and may be similar to [andesitic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Andesitic "Andesitic") rocks on Earth, or silica glass. Regions of low [albedo](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Albedo "Albedo") suggest concentrations of [plagioclase feldspar](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Plagioclase_feldspar "Plagioclase feldspar"), with northern low albedo regions displaying higher than normal concentrations of sheet silicates and high-silicon glass. Parts of the southern highlands include detectable amounts of high-calcium [pyroxenes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pyroxenes "Pyroxenes"). Localized concentrations of [hematite](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hematite "Hematite") and [olivine](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Olivine "Olivine") have been found.[[62]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-jgr107_E6-64) Much of the surface is deeply covered by finely grained [iron(III) oxide](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iron(III)_oxide "Iron(III) oxide") dust.[[63]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-sci300a-65)

The _[Phoenix](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Phoenix\_(spacecraft) "Phoenix (spacecraft)")_ lander returned data showing Martian soil to be slightly alkaline and containing elements such as [magnesium](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Magnesium "Magnesium"), [sodium](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sodium "Sodium"), [potassium](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Potassium "Potassium") and [chlorine](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chlorine "Chlorine"). These nutrients are found in soils on Earth, and are necessary for plant growth.[[64]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-bbc080627-66) Experiments performed by the lander showed that the Martian soil has a [basic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Base_(chemistry) "Base (chemistry)")[pH](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/PH "PH") of 7.7, and contains 0.6% [perchlorate](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Perchlorate "Perchlorate") by weight,[[65]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-67)[[66]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-68) concentrations that are [toxic to humans](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Martian_soil#Toxicity "Martian soil").[[67]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-toxicmars-69)[[68]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-toxicsoil1-70)

[Streaks](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dark_slope_streak "Dark slope streak") are common across Mars and new ones appear frequently on steep slopes of craters, troughs, and valleys. The streaks are dark at first and get lighter with age. The streaks can start in a tiny area, then spread out for hundreds of metres. They have been seen to follow the edges of boulders and other obstacles in their path. The commonly accepted hypotheses include that they are dark underlying layers of soil revealed after avalanches of bright dust or [dust devils](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dust_devil "Dust devil").[[69]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-jpl_dust_devil-71) Several other explanations have been put forward, including those that involve water or even the growth of organisms.[[70]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-gpl29_23_41-72)[[71]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-oleb33_4_515-73)

[Environmental radiation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Environmental_radioactivity "Environmental radioactivity") levels on the surface are on average 0.64[millisieverts](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sievert "Sievert") of radiation per day, and significantly less than the radiation of 1.84 millisieverts per day or 22[millirads](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rad_(radiation_unit) "Rad (radiation unit)") per day during the flight to and from Mars.[[72]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-Williams_2016-74)[[73]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-Wall_2013-75) For comparison the radiation levels in [low Earth orbit](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Low_Earth_orbit "Low Earth orbit"), where Earth's [space stations](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_station "Space station") orbit, are around 0.5 millisieverts of radiation per day.[[74]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-NASA_Jet_Propulsion_Laboratory_(JPL)_2003-76)[Hellas Planitia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hellas_Planitia "Hellas Planitia") has the lowest surface radiation at about 0.342 millisieverts per day, featuring [lava tubes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lava_tube "Lava tube") southwest of [Hadriacus Mons](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hadriacus_Mons "Hadriacus Mons") with potentially levels as low as 0.064 millisieverts per day,[[75]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-Paris_Davies_Tognetti_Zahniser_2020-77) comparable to radiation levels during flights on Earth.

### Magnetic characteristics

Although Mars has no evidence of a structured [global magnetic field](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Magnetic_field_of_Mars "Magnetic field of Mars"),[[76]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-magnetosphere-78) observations show that parts of the planet's crust have been magnetized, suggesting that alternating polarity reversals of its dipole field have occurred in the past. This [paleomagnetism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Paleomagnetism "Paleomagnetism") of magnetically susceptible minerals is similar to the [alternating bands found on Earth's ocean floors](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Plate_tectonics#Magnetic_striping "Plate tectonics"). One hypothesis, published in 1999 and re-examined in October 2005 (with the help of the _[Mars Global Surveyor](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars\_Global\_Surveyor "Mars Global Surveyor")_), is that these bands suggest [plate tectonic activity](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Plate_tectonics "Plate tectonics") on Mars four billion years ago, before the planetary [dynamo](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dynamo_theory "Dynamo theory") ceased to function and the planet's magnetic field faded.[[77]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-plates-79)

Geography and features
----------------------

Further information: [Areoid](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Areoid "Areoid")

Animation showing major features of Mars

Although better remembered for mapping the Moon, [Johann Heinrich von Mädler](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Johann_Heinrich_von_M%C3%A4dler "Johann Heinrich von Mädler") and [Wilhelm Beer](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wilhelm_Beer "Wilhelm Beer") were the first areographers. They began by establishing that most of Mars's surface features were permanent and by more precisely determining the planet's rotation period. In 1840, Mädler combined ten years of observations and drew the first map of Mars.[[78]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-80)

Features on Mars are named from a variety of sources. [Albedo features](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Classical_albedo_features_on_Mars "Classical albedo features on Mars") are named for classical mythology. Craters larger than roughly 50 km are named for deceased scientists and writers and others who have contributed to the study of Mars. Smaller craters are named for towns and villages of the world with populations of less than 100,000. Large valleys are named for the word "Mars" or "star" in various languages; smaller valleys are named for rivers.[[79]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-usgs-81)

Large [albedo](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Albedo "Albedo") features retain many of the older names but are often updated to reflect new knowledge of the nature of the features. For example, _Nix Olympica_ (the snows of Olympus) has become _[Olympus Mons](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Olympus\_Mons "Olympus Mons")_ (Mount Olympus).[[80]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-viking_1950_2000-82) The surface of Mars as seen from Earth is divided into two kinds of areas, with differing albedo. The paler plains covered with dust and sand rich in reddish iron oxides were once thought of as Martian "continents" and given names like [Arabia Terra](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabia_Terra "Arabia Terra") (_land of Arabia_) or [Amazonis Planitia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Amazonis_Planitia "Amazonis Planitia") (_Amazonian plain_). The dark features were thought to be seas, hence their names [Mare Erythraeum](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mare_Erythraeum "Mare Erythraeum"), Mare Sirenum and [Aurorae Sinus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Aurorae_Sinus "Aurorae Sinus"). The largest dark feature seen from Earth is [Syrtis Major Planum](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Syrtis_Major_Planum "Syrtis Major Planum").[[81]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-83) The permanent northern polar ice cap is named [Planum Boreum](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planum_Boreum "Planum Boreum"). The southern cap is called [Planum Australe](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planum_Australe "Planum Australe").[[82]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-84)

[![Image 5](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Mars_MOLA_Topography_by_Fabio_Crameri.png/500px-Mars_MOLA_Topography_by_Fabio_Crameri.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Mars_MOLA_Topography_by_Fabio_Crameri.png)

A [MOLA](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars_Orbiter_Laser_Altimeter "Mars Orbiter Laser Altimeter")-based topographic map showing highlands (light colors) dominating the Southern Hemisphere of Mars, lowlands (dark colors) the northern. Volcanic plateaus delimit regions of the northern plains, whereas the highlands are punctuated by several large impact basins.

Mars's equator is defined by its rotation, but the location of its [Prime Meridian](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_Meridian "Prime Meridian") was specified, as was Earth's (at [Greenwich](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Greenwich "Greenwich")), by choice of an arbitrary point; Mädler and Beer selected a line for their first maps of Mars in 1830. After the spacecraft _[Mariner 9](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mariner\_9 "Mariner 9")_ provided extensive imagery of Mars in 1972, a small crater (later called [Airy-0](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Airy-0 "Airy-0")), located in the [Sinus Meridiani](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sinus_Meridiani "Sinus Meridiani") ("Middle Bay" or "Meridian Bay"), was chosen by [Merton E. Davies](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Merton_E._Davies "Merton E. Davies"), [Harold Masursky](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Harold_Masursky "Harold Masursky"), and [Gérard de Vaucouleurs](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/G%C3%A9rard_de_Vaucouleurs "Gérard de Vaucouleurs") for the definition of 0.0° longitude to coincide with the original selection.[[83]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-85)[[84]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-archinal_caplinger-86)[[85]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-87)

Because Mars has no oceans, and hence no "[sea level](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sea_level "Sea level")", a zero-elevation surface had to be selected as a reference level; this is called the _[areoid](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Areoid "Areoid")_[[86]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-NASAMola2007-88) of Mars, analogous to the terrestrial [geoid](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Geoid "Geoid").[[87]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-ArdalanKarimi2009-89) Zero altitude was defined by the height at which there is 610.5[Pa](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pascal_(unit) "Pascal (unit)") (6.105[mbar](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bar_(unit) "Bar (unit)")) of atmospheric pressure.[[88]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-pers66-90) This pressure corresponds to the [triple point](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Triple_point "Triple point") of water, and it is about 0.6% of the sea level surface pressure on Earth (0.006 atm).[[89]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-lunine99-91)

For mapping purposes, the [United States Geological Survey](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/United_States_Geological_Survey "United States Geological Survey") divides the surface of Mars into [thirty cartographic quadrangles](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_quadrangles_on_Mars "List of quadrangles on Mars"), each named for a classical albedo feature it contains.[[90]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-92) In April 2023, _[The New York Times](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/The\_New\_York\_Times "The New York Times")_ reported an updated global map of Mars based on images from the [Hope spacecraft](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hope_Mars_Mission "Hope Mars Mission").[[91]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-NYT-20230415-93) A related, but much more detailed, global Mars map was released by [NASA](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/NASA "NASA") on 16 April 2023.[[92]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-STD-20230416-94)

### Volcanoes

[![Image 6](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Olympus_Mons_-_ESA_Mars_Express_-_Flickr_-_Andrea_Luck.png/330px-Olympus_Mons_-_ESA_Mars_Express_-_Flickr_-_Andrea_Luck.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Olympus_Mons_-_ESA_Mars_Express_-_Flickr_-_Andrea_Luck.png)

Picture of the tallest volcano on Mars, [Olympus Mons](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Olympus_Mons "Olympus Mons"). It is approximately 550 km (340 mi) across.

The vast upland region [Tharsis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tharsis "Tharsis") contains several massive volcanoes, which include the [shield volcano](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shield_volcano "Shield volcano")[Olympus Mons](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Olympus_Mons "Olympus Mons"). The edifice is over 600 km (370 mi) wide.[[93]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-95)[[94]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-Plescia04-96) Because the mountain is so large, with complex structure at its edges, giving a definite height to it is difficult. Its local relief, from the foot of the cliffs which form its northwest margin to its peak, is over 21 km (13 mi),[[94]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-Plescia04-96) a little over twice the height of [Mauna Kea](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mauna_Kea "Mauna Kea") as measured from its base on the ocean floor. The total elevation change from the plains of [Amazonis Planitia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Amazonis_Planitia "Amazonis Planitia"), over 1,000 km (620 mi) to the northwest, to the summit approaches 26 km (16 mi),[[95]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-Comins-97) roughly three times the height of [Mount Everest](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mount_Everest "Mount Everest"), which in comparison stands at just over 8.8 kilometres (5.5 mi). Consequently, Olympus Mons is either the [tallest or second-tallest mountain in the Solar System](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_tallest_mountains_in_the_Solar_System "List of tallest mountains in the Solar System"); the only known mountain which might be taller is the [Rheasilvia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rheasilvia "Rheasilvia") peak on the asteroid [Vesta](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/4_Vesta "4 Vesta"), at 20–25 km (12–16 mi).[[96]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-Schenk2012-98)

### Impact topography

The [dichotomy of Martian topography](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Martian_dichotomy "Martian dichotomy") is striking: northern plains flattened by lava flows contrast with the southern highlands, pitted and cratered by ancient impacts. It is possible that, four billion years ago, the Northern Hemisphere of Mars was struck by an object one-tenth to two-thirds the size of Earth's [Moon](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon "Moon"). If this is the case, the Northern Hemisphere of Mars would be the site of an [impact crater](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Impact_crater "Impact crater") 10,600 by 8,500 kilometres (6,600 by 5,300 mi) in size, or roughly the area of Europe, Asia, and Australia combined, surpassing [Utopia Planitia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Utopia_Planitia "Utopia Planitia") and the Moon's [South Pole–Aitken basin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/South_Pole%E2%80%93Aitken_basin "South Pole–Aitken basin") as the largest impact crater in the Solar System.[[97]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-99)[[98]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-:6-100)[[99]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-101)

Mars is scarred by 43,000 impact craters with a diameter of 5 kilometres (3.1 mi) or greater.[[100]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-wright03-102) The largest exposed crater is [Hellas](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hellas_Planitia "Hellas Planitia"), which is 2,300 kilometres (1,400 mi) wide and 7,000 metres (23,000 ft) deep, and is a light [albedo feature](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Albedo_feature "Albedo feature") clearly visible from Earth.[[101]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-103)[[102]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-104) There are other notable impact features, such as [Argyre](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Argyre_Planitia "Argyre Planitia"), which is around 1,800 kilometres (1,100 mi) in diameter,[[103]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-105) and [Isidis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Isidis_Planitia "Isidis Planitia"), which is around 1,500 kilometres (930 mi) in diameter.[[104]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-106) Due to the smaller mass and size of Mars, the probability of an object colliding with the planet is about half that of Earth. Mars is located closer to the [asteroid belt](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Asteroid_belt "Asteroid belt"), so it has an increased chance of being struck by materials from that source. Mars is more likely to be struck by short-period [comets](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Comet "Comet"), _i.e._, those that lie within the orbit of [Jupiter](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jupiter "Jupiter").[[105]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-emp9-107)

Martian craters can[_[discuss](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Talk:Mars "Talk:Mars")_] have a morphology that suggests the ground became wet after the meteor impact.[[106]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-emp45-108)

### Tectonic sites

[![Image 7](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Mars_Valles_Marineris.jpeg/250px-Mars_Valles_Marineris.jpeg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Mars_Valles_Marineris.jpeg)

[Valles Marineris](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Valles_Marineris "Valles Marineris"), taken by the _[Viking 1](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Viking\_1 "Viking 1")_ probe

The large canyon, [Valles Marineris](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Valles_Marineris "Valles Marineris") (Latin for '[Mariner](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mariner_program "Mariner program") Valleys, also known as Agathodaemon in the old canal maps[[107]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-109)), has a length of 4,000 kilometres (2,500 mi) and a depth of up to 7 kilometres (4.3 mi). The length of Valles Marineris is equivalent to the length of Europe and extends across one-fifth the circumference of Mars. By comparison, the [Grand Canyon](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Grand_Canyon "Grand Canyon") on Earth is only 446 kilometres (277 mi) long and nearly 2 kilometres (1.2 mi) deep. Valles Marineris was formed due to the swelling of the Tharsis area, which caused the crust in the area of Valles Marineris to collapse. In 2012, it was proposed that Valles Marineris is not just a [graben](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graben "Graben"), but a plate boundary where 150 kilometres (93 mi) of [transverse motion](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Transform_fault "Transform fault") has occurred, making Mars a planet with possibly a two-[tectonic plate](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tectonic_plate "Tectonic plate") arrangement.[[108]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-tectonic-110)[[109]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-Lin,_An-111)

### Holes and caves

Images from the [Thermal Emission Imaging System](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Thermal_Emission_Imaging_System "Thermal Emission Imaging System") (THEMIS) aboard NASA's [Mars Odyssey orbiter](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/2001_Mars_Odyssey "2001 Mars Odyssey") have revealed seven possible [cave](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cave "Cave") entrances on the flanks of the volcano [Arsia Mons](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arsia_Mons "Arsia Mons").[[110]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-cushing_titus_wynn07-112) The caves, named after loved ones of their discoverers, are collectively known as the "seven sisters".[[111]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-nau070328-113) Cave entrances measure from 100 to 252 metres (328 to 827 ft) wide and they are estimated to be at least 73 to 96 metres (240 to 315 ft) deep. Because light does not reach the floor of most of the caves, they may extend much deeper than these lower estimates and widen below the surface. "Dena" is the only exception; its floor is visible and was measured to be 130 metres (430 ft) deep. The interiors of these caverns may be protected from micrometeoroids, UV radiation, [solar flares](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Solar_flare "Solar flare") and high energy particles that bombard the planet's surface.[[112]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-bbc070317-114)[[113]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-115)

Atmosphere
----------

[![Image 8: Image of Mars](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Mars_-_Atmosphere_and_Cassini_Crater_-_Hope_Mission%2C_Orbit_19_%2852252929420%29.png/250px-Mars_-_Atmosphere_and_Cassini_Crater_-_Hope_Mission%2C_Orbit_19_%2852252929420%29.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Mars_-_Atmosphere_and_Cassini_Crater_-_Hope_Mission,_Orbit_19_(52252929420).png)

A broad view of Mars's atmosphere by _Hope_ orbiter

Mars lost its [magnetosphere](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Magnetosphere "Magnetosphere") 4 billion years ago,[[114]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-swind-116) possibly because of numerous asteroid strikes,[[115]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-117) so the [solar wind](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Solar_wind "Solar wind") interacts directly with the Martian [ionosphere](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ionosphere "Ionosphere"), lowering the atmospheric density by [stripping](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Atmospheric_stripping "Atmospheric stripping") away atoms from the outer layer.[[116]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-:7-118) Both _[Mars Global Surveyor](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars\_Global\_Surveyor "Mars Global Surveyor")_ and _[Mars Express](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars\_Express "Mars Express")_ have detected ionized atmospheric particles trailing off into space behind Mars,[[114]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-swind-116)[[117]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-swind2-119) and this atmospheric loss is being studied by the [MAVEN](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/MAVEN "MAVEN") orbiter. Compared to Earth, the [atmosphere](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Celestial_body_atmosphere "Celestial body atmosphere") of Mars is quite rarefied. [Atmospheric pressure](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Atmospheric_pressure "Atmospheric pressure") on the surface today ranges from a low of 30[Pa](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pascal_(unit) "Pascal (unit)") (0.0044[psi](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pound_per_square_inch "Pound per square inch")) on [Olympus Mons](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Olympus_Mons "Olympus Mons") to over 1,155 Pa (0.1675 psi) in [Hellas Planitia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hellas_Planitia "Hellas Planitia"), with a mean pressure at the surface level of 600 Pa (0.087 psi).[[118]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-bolonkin09-120) The highest atmospheric density on Mars is equal to that found 35 kilometres (22 mi)[[119]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-atkinson07-121) above Earth's surface. The resulting mean surface pressure is only 0.6% of Earth's 101.3 kPa (14.69 psi). The [scale height](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Scale_height "Scale height") of the atmosphere is about 10.8 kilometres (6.7 mi),[[120]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-carr06-122) which is higher than Earth's 6 kilometres (3.7 mi), because the surface [gravity of Mars](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gravity_of_Mars "Gravity of Mars") is only about 38% of Earth's.[[121]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-123)

The atmosphere of Mars consists of about 96% [carbon dioxide](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carbon_dioxide "Carbon dioxide"), 1.93% [argon](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Argon "Argon") and 1.89% [nitrogen](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nitrogen "Nitrogen") along with traces of [oxygen](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Oxygen "Oxygen") and water.[[2]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-NasaFactSheet-3)[[122]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-Abundance-124)[[116]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-:7-118) The atmosphere is quite dusty, containing particulates about 1.5 [μm](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/%CE%9Cm "Μm") in diameter which give the [Martian sky](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Extraterrestrial_skies#Mars "Extraterrestrial skies") a [tawny](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tawny_(color) "Tawny (color)") color when seen from the surface.[[123]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-dusty-125) It may take on a [pink](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pink "Pink") hue due to [iron oxide](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iron_oxide "Iron oxide") particles suspended in it.[[22]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-Rees2012-24) The concentration of [methane in the Martian atmosphere](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Methane_on_Mars "Methane on Mars") fluctuates from about 0.24 [ppb](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Parts_per_billion "Parts per billion") during the northern winter to about 0.65 ppb during the summer.[[124]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-Sample2018-126) Estimates of its lifetime range from 0.6 to 4 years,[[125]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-plumes-127)[[126]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-nature460-128) so its presence indicates that an active source of the gas must be present. Methane is most likely produced by non-biological process such as [serpentinization](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Serpentinite "Serpentinite") involving water, carbon dioxide, and the mineral [olivine](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Olivine "Olivine"), which is known to be common on Mars,[[127]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-olivine-129) although it could also be produced by Martian life.[[128]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-130)

[![Image 9](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/PIA18613-MarsMAVEN-Atmosphere-3UV-Views-20141014.jpg/330px-PIA18613-MarsMAVEN-Atmosphere-3UV-Views-20141014.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:PIA18613-MarsMAVEN-Atmosphere-3UV-Views-20141014.jpg)

[Escaping atmosphere](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Atmosphere_of_Mars "Atmosphere of Mars") on Mars ([carbon](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carbon "Carbon"), [oxygen](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Oxygen "Oxygen"), and [hydrogen](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hydrogen "Hydrogen")) by [MAVEN](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/MAVEN "MAVEN") in [UV](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ultraviolet%E2%80%93visible_spectroscopy "Ultraviolet–visible spectroscopy")[[129]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-NASA-20141014-NJ-131)

Compared to Earth, its higher concentration of atmospheric CO 2 and lower surface pressure may be why sound is attenuated more on Mars, where natural sources are rare apart from the wind. Using acoustic recordings collected by the _Perseverance_ rover, researchers concluded that the speed of sound there is approximately 240 m/s for frequencies below 240 Hz, and 250 m/s for those above.[[130]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-132)[[131]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-133)

[Auroras](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Aurora_on_Mars "Aurora on Mars") have been detected on Mars.[[132]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-134)[[133]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-135)[[134]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-NASA-20150318-136) Because Mars lacks a global magnetic field, the types and distribution of auroras there differ from those on Earth;[[135]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-137) rather than being mostly restricted to polar regions as is the case on Earth, a Martian aurora can encompass the planet.[[136]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-:8-138) In September 2017, NASA reported [radiation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Radiation "Radiation") levels on the surface of the planet Mars were temporarily [doubled](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Orders_of_magnitude_(radiation) "Orders of magnitude (radiation)"), and were associated with an [aurora](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Aurora "Aurora") 25 times brighter than any observed earlier, due to a massive, and unexpected, [solar storm](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Coronal_mass_ejection "Coronal mass ejection") in the middle of the month.[[136]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-:8-138)[[137]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-nasa20170929-139)

### Climate

[![Image 10](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Mars_Before_and_During_Global_Dust_Storm.jpg/250px-Mars_Before_and_During_Global_Dust_Storm.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Mars_Before_and_During_Global_Dust_Storm.jpg)

Mars without (on left) and with a global dust storm in July 2001 (on right), including different visible water ice cloud covers, as seen by the Hubble Space Telescope

[![Image 11](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/PIA22546-Mars-AnnualCO2ice-N%26SPoles-20180806.gif/250px-PIA22546-Mars-AnnualCO2ice-N%26SPoles-20180806.gif)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:PIA22546-Mars-AnnualCO2ice-N%26SPoles-20180806.gif)

Rendering of the change of [CO 2 ice](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dry_ice "Dry ice") (not water ice) coverage on the north (left) and south (right) poles of Mars between northern and southern summer.

Mars has seasons, alternating between its northern and southern hemispheres, similar to on Earth. Additionally the orbit of Mars has, compared to Earth's, a large [eccentricity](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Orbital_eccentricity "Orbital eccentricity") and approaches [perihelion](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Apsis "Apsis") when it is summer in its southern hemisphere and winter in its northern, and [aphelion](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Apsis "Apsis") when it is winter in its southern hemisphere and summer in its northern. As a result, the seasons in its southern hemisphere are more extreme and the seasons in its northern are milder than would otherwise be the case. The summer temperatures in the south can be warmer than the equivalent summer temperatures in the north by up to 30°C (54°F).[[138]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-goodman97-140)

Martian surface temperatures vary from lows of about −110°C (−166°F) to highs of up to 35°C (95°F) in equatorial summer.[[16]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-hot-18) The wide range in temperatures is due to the thin atmosphere which cannot store much solar heat, the low atmospheric pressure (about 1% that of the [atmosphere of Earth](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Atmosphere_of_Earth "Atmosphere of Earth")), and the low [thermal inertia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Thermal_inertia "Thermal inertia") of Martian soil.[[139]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-nasa_surface-141) The planet is 1.52 times as far from the Sun as Earth, resulting in just 43% of the amount of sunlight.[[140]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-disc920901-142)[[141]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-143)

Mars has the largest [dust storms](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dust_storm "Dust storm") in the Solar System, reaching speeds of over 160 km/h (100 mph). These can vary from a storm over a small area, to gigantic storms that cover the entire planet. They tend to occur when Mars is closest to the Sun, and have been shown to increase global temperature.[[142]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-philips01-144) Seasons also produce [dry ice](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dry_ice "Dry ice") covering [polar ice caps](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Martian_polar_ice_caps "Martian polar ice caps").[[143]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-a652-145)

Hydrology
---------

[![Image 12](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/PIA26046-Mars-BuriedIceMaps-20231026.jpg/500px-PIA26046-Mars-BuriedIceMaps-20231026.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:PIA26046-Mars-BuriedIceMaps-20231026.jpg)

Mars contains water, though mostly as dust covered polar layers of ice, as mapped in this image.

While Mars contains [water in larger amounts](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Water_on_terrestrial_planets_of_the_Solar_System "Water on terrestrial planets of the Solar System"), most of it is dust covered water ice at the [Martian polar ice caps](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Martian_polar_ice_caps "Martian polar ice caps").[[144]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-specials1-146)[[145]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-bbc040124-147)[[146]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-148)[[147]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-sci299-149)[[148]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-:3-150) The volume of water ice in the south polar ice cap, if melted, would be enough to cover most of the surface of the planet with a depth of 11 metres (36 ft).[[149]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-nasa070315-151)

Water in its liquid form cannot persist on the surface due to Mars' low atmospheric pressure, which is less than 1% that of Earth.[[150]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-nasa.gov-152) Only at the lowest of elevations are the pressure and temperature high enough for liquid water to exist for short periods.[[47]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-mars-in-depth-49)[[151]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-jgr110-153)

Although little water is present in the atmosphere, there is enough to produce clouds of water ice and different cases of [snow](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Snow "Snow") and [frost](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Frost "Frost"), often mixed with snow of [carbon dioxide](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carbon_dioxide "Carbon dioxide")[dry ice](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dry_ice "Dry ice").[[152]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-k104-154)

### Past hydrosphere

[![Image 13](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Artist%E2%80%99s_impression_of_Mars_four_billion_years_ago.jpg/250px-Artist%E2%80%99s_impression_of_Mars_four_billion_years_ago.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Artist%E2%80%99s_impression_of_Mars_four_billion_years_ago.jpg)

Artist’s impression of Mars four billion years ago

[Landforms](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Geomorphology "Geomorphology") visible on Mars strongly suggest that liquid water has existed on the planet's surface. Huge linear swathes of scoured ground, known as [outflow channels](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Outflow_channels "Outflow channels"), cut across the surface in about 25 places. These are thought to be a record of erosion caused by the catastrophic release of water from subsurface aquifers, though some of these structures have been hypothesized to result from the action of glaciers or lava.[[153]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-Kerr2005-155)[[154]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-Jaeger2007-156) One of the larger examples, [Ma'adim Vallis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ma%27adim_Vallis "Ma'adim Vallis"), is 700 kilometres (430 mi) long, much greater than the Grand Canyon, with a width of 20 kilometres (12 mi) and a depth of 2 kilometres (1.2 mi) in places. It is thought to have been carved by flowing water early in Mars's history.[[155]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-lucchita_rosanova-157) The youngest of these channels is thought to have formed only a few million years ago.[[156]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-nature434-158)

Elsewhere, particularly on the oldest areas of the Martian surface, finer-scale, dendritic [networks of valleys](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Valley_networks_(Mars) "Valley networks (Mars)") are spread across significant proportions of the landscape. Features of these valleys and their distribution strongly imply that they were carved by [runoff](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Surface_runoff "Surface runoff") resulting from precipitation in early Mars history. Subsurface water flow and [groundwater sapping](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Groundwater_sapping "Groundwater sapping") may play important subsidiary roles in some networks, but precipitation was probably the root cause of the incision in almost all cases.[[157]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-CraddockHoward2002-159)

Along craters and canyon walls, there are thousands of features that appear similar to terrestrial [gullies](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gullies "Gullies"). The gullies tend to be in the highlands of the Southern Hemisphere and face the Equator; all are poleward of 30° latitude. A number of authors have suggested that their formation process involves liquid water, probably from melting ice,[[158]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-sci288-160)[[159]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-nasa061206-161) although others have argued for formation mechanisms involving carbon dioxide frost or the movement of dry dust.[[160]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-bbc061206-162)[[161]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-nasa061206b-163) No partially degraded gullies have formed by weathering and no superimposed impact craters have been observed, indicating that these are young features, possibly still active.[[159]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-nasa061206-161) Other geological features, such as [deltas](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/River_delta "River delta") and [alluvial fans](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alluvial_fans "Alluvial fans") preserved in craters, are further evidence for warmer, wetter conditions at an interval or intervals in earlier Mars history.[[162]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-Lewis2006-164) Such conditions necessarily require the widespread presence of [crater lakes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Volcanic_crater_lake "Volcanic crater lake") across a large proportion of the surface, for which there is independent mineralogical, sedimentological and geomorphological evidence.[[163]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-Matsubara2011-165) Further evidence that liquid water once existed on the surface of Mars comes from the detection of specific minerals such as [hematite](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hematite "Hematite") and [goethite](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Goethite "Goethite"), both of which sometimes form in the presence of water.[[164]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-nasa040303-166)

### History of observations and findings of water evidence

[![Image 14](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/Perspective_view_of_Korolev_crater.jpg/250px-Perspective_view_of_Korolev_crater.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Perspective_view_of_Korolev_crater.jpg)

As top surface layer water appears readily visible at some places on Mars, as in this polar [crater called Korolev](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Korolev_(Martian_crater) "Korolev (Martian crater)"), seen here in [3D projection](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/3D_projection "3D projection").

In 2004, _Opportunity_ detected the mineral [jarosite](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jarosite "Jarosite"). This forms only in the presence of acidic water, showing that water once existed on Mars.[[165]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-nasa101001-167)[[166]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-168) The _Spirit_ rover found concentrated deposits of [silica](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Silica "Silica") in 2007 that indicated wet conditions in the past, and in December 2011, the mineral [gypsum](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gypsum "Gypsum"), which also forms in the presence of water, was found on the surface by NASA's Mars rover Opportunity.[[167]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-169)[[168]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-nasa-170)[[169]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-nationalgeographic-171) It is estimated that the amount of water in the upper mantle of Mars, represented by [hydroxyl ions](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hydroxyl_ion "Hydroxyl ion") contained within Martian minerals, is equal to or greater than that of Earth at 50–300 parts per million of water, which is enough to cover the entire planet to a depth of 200–1,000 metres (660–3,280 ft).[[170]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-nationalgeographic1-172)[[171]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-173)

On 18 March 2013, [NASA](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/NASA "NASA") reported evidence from instruments on the [_Curiosity_ rover](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Curiosity_(rover) "Curiosity (rover)") of [mineral hydration](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mineral_hydration "Mineral hydration"), likely hydrated [calcium sulfate](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Calcium_sulfate "Calcium sulfate"), in several [rock samples](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rock_(geology) "Rock (geology)") including the broken fragments of ["Tintina" rock](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tintina_(rock) "Tintina (rock)") and ["Sutton Inlier" rock](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_rocks_on_Mars#Curiosity "List of rocks on Mars") as well as in [veins](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vein_(geology) "Vein (geology)") and [nodules](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nodule_(geology) "Nodule (geology)") in other rocks like ["Knorr" rock](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_rocks_on_Mars#Curiosity "List of rocks on Mars") and ["Wernicke" rock](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_rocks_on_Mars#Curiosity "List of rocks on Mars").[[172]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-NASA-20130318-174)[[173]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-BBC-20130319-175) Analysis using the rover's [DAN instrument](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Curiosity_(rover)#Dynamic_Albedo_of_Neutrons_(DAN) "Curiosity (rover)") provided evidence of subsurface water, amounting to as much as 4% water content, down to a depth of 60 centimetres (24 in), during the rover's traverse from the _[Bradbury Landing](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bradbury\_Landing "Bradbury Landing")_ site to the _Yellowknife Bay_ area in the _[Glenelg](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glenelg,\_Mars "Glenelg, Mars")_ terrain.[[172]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-NASA-20130318-174) In September 2015, NASA announced that they had found strong evidence of hydrated [brine](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Brine "Brine") flows in [recurring slope lineae](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Recurring_slope_lineae "Recurring slope lineae"), based on spectrometer readings of the darkened areas of slopes.[[174]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-176)[[175]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-177)[[176]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-Ojhaetal2015-178) These streaks flow downhill in Martian summer, when the temperature is above −23°C, and freeze at lower temperatures.[[177]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-179) These observations supported earlier hypotheses, based on timing of formation and their rate of growth, that these dark streaks resulted from water flowing just below the surface.[[178]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-SeasonalFlows-180) However, later work suggested that the lineae may be dry, granular flows instead, with at most a limited role for water in initiating the process.[[179]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-181) A definitive conclusion about the presence, extent, and role of liquid water on the Martian surface remains elusive.[[180]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-182)[[181]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-183)

Researchers suspect much of the low northern plains of the planet were [covered with an ocean](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars_ocean_hypothesis "Mars ocean hypothesis") hundreds of meters deep, though this theory remains controversial.[[182]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-Head1999-184) In March 2015, scientists stated that such an ocean might have been the size of Earth's [Arctic Ocean](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arctic_Ocean "Arctic Ocean"). This finding was derived from the ratio of [protium](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Protium_(isotope) "Protium (isotope)") to [deuterium](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Deuterium "Deuterium") in the modern Martian atmosphere compared to that ratio on Earth. The amount of Martian deuterium (D/H = 9.3 ± 1.7 10−4) is five to seven times the amount on Earth (D/H = 1.56 10−4), suggesting that ancient Mars had significantly higher levels of water. Results from the _Curiosity_ rover had previously found a high ratio of deuterium in [Gale Crater](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gale_Crater "Gale Crater"), though not significantly high enough to suggest the former presence of an ocean. Other scientists caution that these results have not been confirmed, and point out that Martian climate models have not yet shown that the planet was warm enough in the past to support bodies of liquid water.[[183]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-NYT-20150305-185) Near the northern polar cap is the 81.4 kilometres (50.6 mi) wide [Korolev Crater](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Korolev_(Martian_crater) "Korolev (Martian crater)"), which the [Mars Express](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars_Express "Mars Express") orbiter found to be filled with approximately 2,200 cubic kilometres (530 cu mi) of water ice.[[184]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-TG1218-186)

In November 2016, NASA reported finding a large amount of underground ice in the [Utopia Planitia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Utopia_Planitia "Utopia Planitia") region. The volume of water detected has been estimated to be equivalent to the volume of water in [Lake Superior](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lake_Superior "Lake Superior") (which is 12,100 cubic kilometers[[185]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-187)).[[186]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-188)[[187]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-NASA-20161122-189) During observations from 2018 through 2021, the ExoMars Trace Gas Orbiter spotted indications of water, probably subsurface ice, in the Valles Marineris canyon system.[[188]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-190)

Orbital motion
--------------

[![Image 15: Mars circling the Sun further and slower than Earth](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Solar_system_orrery_inner_planets.gif/330px-Solar_system_orrery_inner_planets.gif)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Solar_system_orrery_inner_planets.gif)

Orbit of Mars and other Inner Solar System planets

Mars's average distance from the Sun is roughly 230 million km (143 million mi), and its orbital period is 687 (Earth) days. The solar day (or [sol](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars_sol "Mars sol")) on Mars is only slightly longer than an Earth day: 24 hours, 39 minutes, and 35.244 seconds.[[189]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-191) A Martian year is equal to 1.8809 Earth years, or 1 year, 320 days, and 18.2 hours.[[2]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-NasaFactSheet-3) The [gravitational potential](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gravitational_potential "Gravitational potential") difference and thus the [delta-v needed](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Delta-v_budget "Delta-v budget") to transfer between Mars and Earth is the second lowest for Earth.[[190]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-Petropoulos_Longuski_Bonfiglio_2000_pp._776%E2%80%93783-192)[[191]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-Taylor_2020-193)

The axial tilt of Mars is 25.19° relative to its [orbital plane](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Orbital_plane "Orbital plane"), which is similar to the [axial tilt of Earth](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ecliptic#Obliquity_of_the_ecliptic "Ecliptic").[[2]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-NasaFactSheet-3) As a result, Mars has seasons like Earth, though on Mars they are nearly twice as long because its orbital period is that much longer. In the present day, the orientation of the [north pole](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/North_pole "North pole") of Mars is close to the star [Deneb](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Deneb "Deneb").[[21]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-barlow08-23)

Mars has a relatively pronounced [orbital eccentricity](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Orbital_eccentricity "Orbital eccentricity") of about 0.09; of the seven other planets in the Solar System, only [Mercury](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mercury_(planet) "Mercury (planet)") has a larger orbital eccentricity. It is known that in the past, Mars has had a much more circular orbit. At one point, 1.35 million Earth years ago, Mars had an eccentricity of roughly 0.002, much less than that of Earth today.[[192]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-mars_eccentricity-194) Mars's [cycle of eccentricity](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milankovitch_cycles "Milankovitch cycles") is 96,000 Earth years compared to Earth's cycle of 100,000 years.[[193]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-Meeus2003-195)

Mars has its closest approach to Earth ([opposition](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Opposition_(astronomy) "Opposition (astronomy)")) in a [synodic period](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Synodic_period "Synodic period") of 779.94 days. It should not be confused with Mars [conjunction](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Conjunction_(astronomy) "Conjunction (astronomy)"), where the Earth and Mars are at opposite sides of the Solar System and form a straight line crossing the Sun. The average time between the successive oppositions of Mars, its [synodic period](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Synodic_period "Synodic period"), is 780 days; but the number of days between successive oppositions can range from 764 to 812.[[193]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-Meeus2003-195) The distance at close approach varies between about 54 and 103 million km (34 and 64 million mi) due to the planets' [elliptical](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ellipse "Ellipse") orbits, which causes comparable variation in [angular size](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Angular_size "Angular size").[[194]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-Laskar2003-196) At their furthest Mars and Earth can be as far as 401 million km (249 million mi) apart.[[195]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-m455-197) Mars comes into opposition from Earth every 2.1 years. The planets come into opposition near Mars's [perihelion](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Perihelion "Perihelion") in 2003, 2018 and 2035, with the 2020 and 2033 events being particularly close to perihelic opposition.[[196]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-:1-198)[[197]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-199)[[198]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-200)

[![Image 16](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Mars_-_2020_Opposition_%28crop%29.jpg/250px-Mars_-_2020_Opposition_%28crop%29.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Mars_-_2020_Opposition_(crop).jpg)

Mars seen through a 16-inch amateur telescope, at 2020 opposition

The mean [apparent magnitude](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Apparent_magnitude "Apparent magnitude") of Mars is +0.71 with a standard deviation of 1.05.[[19]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-Mallama_and_Hilton-21) Because the orbit of Mars is eccentric, the magnitude at [opposition](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Opposition_(planets) "Opposition (planets)") from the Sun can range from about −3.0 to −1.4.[[199]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-MallamaSky-201) The minimum brightness is magnitude +1.86 when the planet is near [aphelion](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Apsis "Apsis") and in [conjunction](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Conjunction_(astronomy) "Conjunction (astronomy)") with the Sun.[[19]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-Mallama_and_Hilton-21) At its brightest, Mars (along with [Jupiter](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jupiter "Jupiter")) is second only to Venus in apparent brightness.[[19]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-Mallama_and_Hilton-21) Mars usually appears distinctly yellow, orange, or red. When farthest away from Earth, it is more than seven times farther away than when it is closest. Mars is usually close enough for particularly good viewing once or twice at 15-year or 17-year intervals.[[200]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-202) Optical ground-based telescopes are typically limited to resolving features about 300 kilometres (190 mi) across when Earth and Mars are closest because of Earth's atmosphere.[[201]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-usra-203)

As Mars approaches opposition, it begins a period of [retrograde motion](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Apparent_retrograde_motion "Apparent retrograde motion"), which means it will appear to move backwards in a looping curve with respect to the background stars. This retrograde motion lasts for about 72 days, and Mars reaches its peak apparent brightness in the middle of this interval.[[202]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-zeilik02-204)

Moons
-----

[![Image 17](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Phobos_colour_2008.jpg/250px-Phobos_colour_2008.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Phobos_colour_2008.jpg)

[![Image 18](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Deimos-MRO.jpg/250px-Deimos-MRO.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Deimos-MRO.jpg)

Enhanced-color HiRISE image of [Deimos](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Deimos_(moon) "Deimos (moon)") (not to scale), showing its smooth blanket of [regolith](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Regolith "Regolith")

Mars has two relatively small (compared to Earth's) natural moons, [Phobos](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Phobos_(moon) "Phobos (moon)") (about 22 kilometres (14 mi) in diameter) and [Deimos](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Deimos_(moon) "Deimos (moon)") (about 12 kilometres (7.5 mi) in diameter), which orbit close to the planet. The origin of both moons is unclear, although a popular theory states that they were asteroids captured into Martian orbit.[[203]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-esa31031-205)

Both satellites were discovered in 1877 by [Asaph Hall](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Asaph_Hall "Asaph Hall") and were named after the characters [Phobos](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Phobos_(mythology) "Phobos (mythology)") (the deity of panic and fear) and [Deimos](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Deimos_(mythology) "Deimos (mythology)") (the deity of terror and dread), twins from [Greek mythology](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Greek_mythology "Greek mythology") who accompanied their father [Ares](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ares "Ares"), god of war, into battle.[[204]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-206)[Mars](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars_(mythology) "Mars (mythology)") was the Roman equivalent to Ares. In modern [Greek](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Greek_language "Greek language"), the planet retains its ancient name _Ares_ (Aris: _Άρης_).[[98]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-:6-100)

From the surface of Mars, the motions of Phobos and Deimos appear different from that of the Earth's satellite, the [Moon](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon "Moon"). Phobos rises in the west, sets in the east, and rises again in just 11 hours. Deimos, being only just outside [synchronous orbit](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Synchronous_orbit "Synchronous orbit")– where the orbital period would match the planet's period of rotation– rises as expected in the east, but slowly. Because the orbit of Phobos is below a synchronous altitude, [tidal forces](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tidal_force "Tidal force") from Mars are gradually lowering its orbit. In about 50 million years, it could either crash into Mars's surface or break up into a ring structure around the planet.[[205]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars#cite_note-207)

The origin of the two satellites is not well understood. Their low albedo and [carbonaceous chondrite](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carbonaceous_chondrite "Carbonaceous chondrite") composition have been regarded as similar to asteroids, supporting a capture theory. The unstable orbit of Phobos would seem to point toward a relatively recent capture. But both have [circular orbits](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Circular_orbit "Circular orbit") near the equator, which is unusual for captured objects, and the required capture dynamics are complex.
